Why Non-Action Cannot Be Attributed to an Agent
The author argues that non-action (tark) cannot be caused by an agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the common definition of a capable agent as one who can act or not act is problematic from a kalam perspective.

Core Idea
Non-existence cannot be produced, so an agent cannot 'cause' non-action.

In this passage, the author critically examines a widely accepted definition of the capable agent (al-qadir). He argues that the ability to 'not act' cannot be attributed to an agent because non-existence is pure negation and cannot be effected by power. This forces us to rethink what it means to be an agent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's flow come through.
2
Identify the two reasons against remaining non-existence
The author gives two reasons why remaining non-existence (al-`adam al-baqi) cannot be attributed to an agent. Find them and note how each reason independently blocks the attribution.
The two reasons are: (1) it is non-existential, (2) it is a continuation (not a new act).
3
Reflect on the conclusion about 'tark'
After establishing that remaining non-existence cannot be caused, the author concludes that 'tark' (leaving) is just continuation of original non-existence, so the agent has no power over it. Consider why this makes the initial definition problematic.
This conclusion challenges the very definition of a capable agent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البحث الرابع: إن قول القائل: القادر هو الذي يصح منه أن يفعل وأن لا يفعل بحسب الدواعي المختلفة كلام مشكل. وذلك لأن قولنا: إنه لم يفعل: إشارة إلى أنه نفي للعدم الأصلي، كما كان. والعلم يمتنع وقوعه بالفاعل. لأن القدرة صفة مؤثرة، والعدم نفي محض، وصلب صرف فالقول بأن العدم وقع بالفاعل والقادر محال. وأيضاً: الشيء حال بقائه يمتنع إسناده إلى الفاعل. لما ثبت أن تكوين الكائن محال. وإذا كان الأمر كذلك فالعدم الباقي يمتنع إسناده إلى القادر. لوجهين: الأول: إن كونه عدمياً يمنع من إسناده إلى الفاعل. والثاني: إن كونه باقياً يمنع أيضاً من إسناده إلى الفاعل. فيثبت أن العدم الباقي يمتنع استناده إلى القادر من هذين الوجهين. ولما ثبت أن الترك لا معنى له. إلا البقاء على العدم الأصلي، وثبت أن البقاء على العدم الأصلي يمتنع إسناده إلى الفاعل يثبت حينئذ: أن القادر لا قدرة له على الترك البتة. وإذا كان كذلك كان قوله: القادر هو الذي يصح منه الفعل والترك قولاً مشكلاً. ولهذا الكلام مزيد غور.
Fourth Investigation: The statement of one who says: 'The capable agent (al-qadir) is he from whom it is valid to act and to not act according to different motives' is problematic. That is because our saying 'he did not act' indicates a negation (nafy) of the original non-existence (al-`adam al-asli), as it was. And knowledge prevents its occurrence by an agent. Because power is an effective attribute, and non-existence is pure negation, sheer nothingness, so the statement that non-existence occurs by an agent and a capable one is impossible. Also: a thing in its state of remaining (baqa') is impossible to be attributed to an agent, because it has been established that bringing-into-being of an existent is impossible. If this is so, then remaining non-existence (al-`adam al-baqi) is impossible to attribute to the capable agent for two reasons: first, because it is non-existential, that prevents attributing it to an agent; second, because it is remaining, that also prevents attributing it to an agent. So it is established that remaining non-existence is impossible to ascribe to the capable agent from these two aspects. And since it is established that 'leaving' (al-tark) has no meaning except the continuation of original non-existence, and it is established that the continuation of original non-existence is impossible to attribute to an agent, then it is established that the capable agent has absolutely no power over leaving (al-tark). If this is so, then his statement 'the capable agent is he from whom both act and leaving are valid' is a problematic statement. This speech has further depth.
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage challenges the common definition of a capable agent (al-qadir) as one who can both act and refrain from acting. The author argues this is problematic. He reasons: if someone did not act, that means the original non-existence (the state before creation) remains. But non-existence is pure negation and cannot be produced by an agent, because power is an attribute that effects existence, not non-existence. Furthermore, even a thing that continues to exist cannot be attributed to an agent after its first creation, because bringing-into-being is not ongoing. Therefore, the continuation of non-existence (al-`adam al-baqi) cannot be attributed to an agent for two reasons: it is non-existential, and it is a continuation (not a new act). Since 'leaving' (tark) means merely continuing in the original non-existence, and that continuation cannot be attributed to an agent, it follows that the agent has no power over leaving at all. Thus defining the capable agent by the ability to act or not act is problematic. The author hints that this argument has deeper implications.

Key Terms
القادر
العدم الأصلي
العدم الباقي
الترك
القدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That not acting is a positive act of will that can be attributed to an agent.
Correction: The author shows that non-action (tark) is merely the absence of action and the continuation of original non-existence, which cannot be caused by an agent since power only effects existence.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say that the capable agent has no power over 'tark' (leaving)?
A) Because leaving is an action.
B) Because leaving is a continuation of non-existence, which cannot be caused.
C) Because leaving is the opposite of action.
D) Because power is only for creation.

Key Takeaway
The capable agent cannot be defined by the ability to not act because non-action is merely the continuation of original non-existence, which cannot be caused by an agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever excused a sin by thinking 'I just couldn't help it'? This lesson shows that not acting is not a positive act. But when you fail to do good, are you truly powerless, or is your 'leaving' a choice that you are responsible for? Reflect on a specific good deed you left undone today. Will you commit to performing it tomorrow?
?
This lesson proves that God's power does not include not acting. Yet we often doubt God's action in our lives when we see evil or suffering. How does this truth about divine agency challenge your own trust in God's perfect action? What area of your life do you need to surrender to His active control?
?
The author says that calling leaving an act of the capable agent is 'problematic'. Have you ever excused your own inaction by saying 'I could have, but I chose not to'? This lesson forces you to see that your 'not doing' is not an act of your will but mere absence. How will you now take responsibility for what you have left undone?

Classification of Belief and the Equality Claim
Sanusi classifies belief into certain and uncertain and states equality claim with proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi classifies belief into certain and uncertain and why he argues that certain belief equals knowledge in motivating action.

Core Idea
Certain belief, when equally firm, drives action as strongly as knowledge.

Sanusi begins the fourth investigation by clarifying the relationship between belief and knowledge. He first distinguishes between certain and uncertain belief, then makes a bold claim: certain belief is equal to knowledge in its power to motivate or deter. This section establishes the foundation for the rest of the discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening classification
Notice how Sanusi divides belief into two types: certain and uncertain. What defines the first type?
2
Identify the claim
Sanusi states that certain belief equals knowledge in being a motivator and deterrent. Underline this statement.
3
Follow the proof
The proof is based on a comparison of internal feeling. If no difference in certainty, then no difference in effect.
This is a simple psychological argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الرابع: في أن أي هذه الثلاثة أقوى. وأيها أضعف ؟ ونعين أولاً حال العلم مع الاعتقاد ( فنقول : المعتقد في الشيء قد يكون جازماً بكونه محقاً في ذلك الاعتقاد ) وقد لا يكون كذلك . أما القسم الأول وهو ما إذا اعتقدنا اعتقاداً جازماً بأن الأمر كما اعتقده ، فهذا الاعتقاد يساوي العلم في كونه داعياً وصارفاً . والدليل عليه : أن هذا الرجل إذا كان لا يجد في قلبه تفاوتاً بين هذا الاعتقاد الجازم ، وبين العلم في قوة الجزم والثبات ، وجب أن يكون تأثيره في الدعاء إلى الفعل تأثيراً واحداً. فإن قالوا : أليس أن هذا المعتقد إذا شكك نفسه أمكنه ذلك . وأما العالم فإنه ليس كذلك. وإذا كان الأمر كذلك ، فكيف يصح القول بكونهما متساويين في قوة الدعاء إلى الفعل ؟ والجواب : إن الأمر وإن كان كما ذكرتم إلا أنه ما دامت نفسه باقية على ذلك الجزم المساوي لما في العلم من الجزم ، وجب أن تكون الحال واحدة في قوة الدعاء إلى الفعل
Fourth Investigation: Which of these three is strongest and which is weakest? And we first consider the case of knowledge with belief. So we say: The believer in a thing may be certain that he is correct in that belief, or he may not be so. As for the first type, when we believe with certainty that the matter is as we believe it, this belief equals knowledge in being a motivator and a deterrent. The evidence for that: This man, if he finds no difference in his heart between this certain belief and knowledge in strength of certainty and firmness, then necessarily his effect in motivating action would be one. If they say: 'Is it not that this believer, if he doubts himself, can do so? But the knower cannot. If that is the case, how can it be correct to say they are equal in strength of motivation?' The answer: The matter, even if as you mentioned, as long as his soul remains upon that certainty equal to the certainty found in knowledge, then the state must be one in strong motivation.
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage begins a new investigation (المبحث الرابع) into which of three things (knowledge, certain belief, uncertain belief) is strongest and which weakest. First, the author examines the relationship between knowledge and belief. He classifies belief into two types: certain (جازم) and uncertain. For the certain type, he asserts that it equals knowledge in its power to motivate action (داعياً) and to deter (صارفاً). The proof is straightforward: if a person feels no difference in certainty between his belief and knowledge, then their motivational effect must be identical. An objector raises a problem: a believer can be shaken in his belief by self-doubt, whereas a knower cannot. Therefore, how can they be equal? The answer is that as long as the believer remains in that state of certainty equal to knowledge, the motivational force is indeed the same. The possibility of later doubt does not affect the present state. This answer preserves the equality claim while acknowledging the potential fragility of belief.

Key Terms
اعتقاد
علم
جازم
داعٍ
صارف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Belief is always weaker than knowledge in motivating action.
Correction: Sanusi shows that certain belief, when equally firm, has the same motivational force as knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what condition makes certain belief equal to knowledge in motivating action?
A) When the belief is based on scriptural evidence
B) When the believer finds no difference between his belief and knowledge in strength of certainty
C) When the belief leads to the same action as knowledge would
D) When the believer cannot doubt himself at any time

Key Takeaway
Certain belief, when equally firm, has the same motivational strength as knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Is there a belief you hold about your faith that you treat as less certain than knowledge? What would change in your life if you held it with the same firmness as you hold the reality of the sky above you?
?
If your conviction about the Hereafter were as strong as your knowledge that fire burns, how would you spend your time differently starting today? What is the evidence in your daily choices that your belief is not yet at that level?
?
Sanusi says certain belief equals knowledge in motivating action. Think of one action you have delayed because you lacked firm belief. What would it take for you to remove all doubt and act as if you already know for sure?

Objection and Reply: Can Believers Be Shaken?
Sanusi addresses an objection: believers can doubt themselves, so how can belief equal knowledge? He replies that as long as certainty remains, motivation is equal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection against the equality claim and how Sanusi defends it by distinguishing present state from future potential.

Core Idea
Present certainty, not future doubt, determines motivational strength.

After asserting that certain belief equals knowledge, Sanusi anticipates an objection: believers can be shaken by doubt, but knowers cannot. How can they be equal? His reply sharpens the original claim by emphasizing the present state as long as certainty lasts. This interaction clarifies the nature of certainty in Islamic epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Identify the challenger's argument: believers can doubt themselves, so their conviction is not as firm as knowledge.
2
Understand the answer
Sanusi concedes the possibility of doubt but insists that as long as certainty remains, the motivational effect is identical.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this answer preserves the equality claim while acknowledging the fragility of belief.
This shows the importance of present state in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الرابع: في أن أي هذه الثلاثة أقوى. وأيها أضعف ؟ ونعين أولاً حال العلم مع الاعتقاد ( فنقول : المعتقد في الشيء قد يكون جازماً بكونه محقاً في ذلك الاعتقاد ) وقد لا يكون كذلك . أما القسم الأول وهو ما إذا اعتقدنا اعتقاداً جازماً بأن الأمر كما اعتقده ، فهذا الاعتقاد يساوي العلم في كونه داعياً وصارفاً . والدليل عليه : أن هذا الرجل إذا كان لا يجد في قلبه تفاوتاً بين هذا الاعتقاد الجازم ، وبين العلم في قوة الجزم والثبات ، وجب أن يكون تأثيره في الدعاء إلى الفعل تأثيراً واحداً. فإن قالوا : أليس أن هذا المعتقد إذا شكك نفسه أمكنه ذلك . وأما العالم فإنه ليس كذلك. وإذا كان الأمر كذلك ، فكيف يصح القول بكونهما متساويين في قوة الدعاء إلى الفعل ؟ والجواب : إن الأمر وإن كان كما ذكرتم إلا أنه ما دامت نفسه باقية على ذلك الجزم المساوي لما في العلم من الجزم ، وجب أن تكون الحال واحدة في قوة الدعاء إلى الفعل
Fourth Investigation: Which of these three is strongest and which is weakest? And we first consider the case of knowledge with belief. So we say: The believer in a thing may be certain that he is correct in that belief, or he may not be so. As for the first type, when we believe with certainty that the matter is as we believe it, this belief equals knowledge in being a motivator and a deterrent. The evidence for that: This man, if he finds no difference in his heart between this certain belief and knowledge in strength of certainty and firmness, then necessarily his effect in motivating action would be one. If they say: 'Is it not that this believer, if he doubts himself, can do so? But the knower cannot. If that is the case, how can it be correct to say they are equal in strength of motivation?' The answer: The matter, even if as you mentioned, as long as his soul remains upon that certainty equal to the certainty found in knowledge, then the state must be one in strong motivation.
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage begins a new investigation (المبحث الرابع) into which of three things (knowledge, certain belief, uncertain belief) is strongest and which weakest. First, the author examines the relationship between knowledge and belief. He classifies belief into two types: certain (جازم) and uncertain. For the certain type, he asserts that it equals knowledge in its power to motivate action (داعياً) and to deter (صارفاً). The proof is straightforward: if a person feels no difference in certainty between his belief and knowledge, then their motivational effect must be identical. An objector raises a problem: a believer can be shaken in his belief by self-doubt, whereas a knower cannot. Therefore, how can they be equal? The answer is that as long as the believer remains in that state of certainty equal to knowledge, the motivational force is indeed the same. The possibility of later doubt does not affect the present state. This answer preserves the equality claim while acknowledging the potential fragility of belief.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Because believers can doubt later, their belief is never equal to knowledge in motivation.
Correction: Sanusi shows that present certainty suffices; future doubt does not diminish current motivational strength.

Checkpoint
How does Sanusi respond to the objection that believers can doubt themselves?
A) He denies that believers can doubt themselves
B) He says that even if doubt occurs, belief still equals knowledge
C) He argues that as long as the believer remains certain, the motivational effect is the same
D) He changes his claim and says belief is weaker

Key Takeaway
The possibility of future doubt does not diminish the present motivational force of certain belief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Is your current certainty about Allah's attributes enough to move you to action—or do you rely on future intentions? What would it take for you to say that your present state of certainty is equal to that of a scholar?
?
If you were to face a doubt about a fundamental belief, would your actions change? Does your current practice reflect the strength of your certainty, or are you merely coasting on habit?
?
Sanusi says as long as your soul remains in certainty, motivation is equal. Are you actively guarding your certainty, or are you neglecting it? What specific step will you take today to reinforce your certainty in the core truths of your faith?

Knowledge vs. Belief: The Two Possibilities of Doubt
Al-Matalib opens the argument by classifying beliefs into two logical possibilities regarding doubt.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument that knowledge is inherently stronger than belief in motivating action, based on whether the belief can be doubted or not.

Core Idea
Knowledge is stronger because it resists doubt, while belief may be doubted.

This lesson presents the opening argument of the passage, where the author categorizes beliefs into two types: those that invite doubt and those that do not. By analyzing these possibilities, he establishes that knowledge is superior in motivational strength. The reasoning hinges on the necessary connection between cause and effect—a stronger cause yields a stronger effect.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification of belief
The author divides belief into two types: one that can be doubted by its holder and one that cannot. Read the Arabic and translation carefully to see this distinction.
This classification is the foundation of the argument.
2
Understand the first possibility
If belief can be doubted, then there is a difference in strength compared to knowledge. Since cause strength determines effect strength, motivation will differ proportionally.
3
Examine the second possibility
If belief cannot be doubted, then no difference in strength remains, but this is false because knowledge provides more secure repose of the soul.
The author asserts this is known by necessity.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The argument concludes that knowledge is always stronger than belief, as the only viable scenario is the first.
This sets up the rest of the discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولمن زعم أن العلم أقوى في الدعاء إلى الفعل من الاعتقاد ، أن يقول : هذا الاعتقاد إما أن يكون بحيث لو شكك صاحبه نفسه فيه أمكنه التشكيك فيه ، أو لا يكون كذلك. فإن كان الأول فحينئذ حصل التفاوت بين العلم وبين هذا الاعتقاد في القوة ، فوجب أن يحصل التفاوت في الدعاء إلى الفعل ، لأن من المعلوم بالضرورة : أن المؤثر كلما كان أقوى ، كان التأثير أقوى ، وإن كان الثاني فحينئذ لا يبقى بين هذا الاعتقاد وبين العلم تفاوت في القوة ، وذلك معلوم البطلان بالضرورة ، لأن من المعلوم بالضرورة . أن سكون النفس إلى العلم الحق ، أيمن من سكونها إلى الاعتقاد الخالي عن الموجب وأما القسم الثاني : وهو المعتقد الذي لا يكون جازماً ، بل يكون كالمضطرب في ذلك الاعتقاد ، فنقول : إن مثل هذا الاعتقاد لا يكون مساوياً للعلم في الدعاء إلى الفعل ، بل يكون أضعف منه . وتقريره : أن الأثر فرع على المؤثر . ولما كان أحد المؤثرين أضعف من الآخر ، كان أثر الضعيف أضعف لا محالة من أثر القوي . فكان دعاء الاعتقاد الضعيف إلى الفعل أضعف من دعاء العلم القوي إليه . وأيضاً : فالعالم الجازم يكون الفعل على النفع الزائد لا يخاف فيها يعلمه أن يتغير ، وأما المعتقد المضطرب فإنه لا بد وأن يجوز أن ينكشف أن الأمر بخلاف ما اعتقده . فحيل لابد وأن يضعف ذلك الاقتضاء واعلم : أنا ربما وجدنا المبطل أو المقلد مقدم على الفعل إقداماً أقوى من إقدام العالم وذلك لا يقدح في قولنا : إن اقتضاء العلم أقوى اقتضاء من الاعتقاد ، وذلك لأن تلك القوة إنما حصلت لأمور أخرى تقارن ذلك الاعتقاد ، ولو لم يحصل إلا ذلك الاعتقاد لما حصلت تلك المزية . وتلك الأمور المقارنة بذلك الإقدام أشياء: أحدها : مدح الناس له في نصرة ذلك القول أو في الإقدام على ذلك العمل . وثانيها : أنه ربما توصل بذلك الإقدام إلى جر أنواع من المنافع العاجلة أو يتخلص بسبب ذلك الفعل من أنواع كثيرة من الذم وثالثها : أن طول الإلف والعادة قد يحمله على ذلك العمل . فهذا هو الكلام في المقايسة الحاصلة بين العلم وبين الاعتقاد
For whoever claims that knowledge is stronger in motivating action than belief, he may say: This belief is either such that if its holder doubts himself, he can cast doubt therein, or not. If the first, then there arises a difference between knowledge and this belief in strength, and necessarily a difference in motivation, because it is known by necessity that the stronger the cause, the stronger the effect. If the second, then no difference remains between this belief and knowledge in strength, and that is known to be false by necessity, because it is known by necessity that the soul's reliance on true knowledge is more secure than its reliance on belief devoid of compulsion. As for the second type: the believer who is not certain but is rather like one agitated in that belief, we say: Such belief is not equal to knowledge in motivating action; rather, it is weaker than it. Its explanation: The effect is subordinate to the cause. Since one of the causes is weaker than the other, the effect of the weak one is inevitably weaker than the effect of the strong one. So the motivation of weak belief to action is weaker than the motivation of strong knowledge to it. Also: The certain knower acts for a great benefit, not fearing that what he knows will change, whereas the agitated believer must allow that it may become clear that the matter is contrary to what he believed. Hence, inevitably, that inclination weakens. And know: We often find the misguided or the blind follower advancing towards an action with a stronger impetus than the advance of the knower, and that does not detract from our statement that the inclination of knowledge is stronger than the inclination of belief, because that strength arises from other things that accompany that belief; if nothing but that belief existed, that superiority would not occur. Those accompanying things for that advance are: First, people's praise for him in supporting that opinion or in undertaking that action. Second, that he might, through that advance, attain various kinds of immediate benefits or escape numerous types of blame. Third, that long-term familiarity and habit may drive him to that action. This is the discourse concerning the comparison between knowledge and belief.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage continues the investigation by presenting a case for those who hold that knowledge is stronger than belief in motivating action. The argument is structured around two possibilities for any belief: either it can be doubted (shakkaka nafsahu) or it cannot. If it can be doubted, then there is a real difference in strength, and thus motivation will differ proportionally. If it cannot be doubted, then no difference remains, but this is deemed false by necessity, since reliance on true knowledge is more secure than reliance on belief without compelling reason. The author then turns to the second type of belief (uncertain, agitated belief). Such belief is plainly weaker than knowledge: the effect follows the cause, so a weaker cause yields a weaker effect. Moreover, the knower acts without fear of change, whereas the agitated believer must admit the possibility of being wrong, which weakens the inclination. The author then addresses an empirical objection: we often see misguided or blindly following people acting with stronger drive than knowers. This, he explains, does not undermine the claim that knowledge's inclination is inherently stronger, because in those cases external factors amplify the motivation: praise, immediate benefits, avoidance of blame, and long-standing habit. Thus the comparison between knowledge and belief remains sound.

Key Terms
اعتقاد (belief)
علم (knowledge)
دعاء (motivation)
مؤثر (cause/effective factor)
سكون النفس (repose of the soul)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a belief cannot be doubted, it is equal to knowledge in strength.
Correction: The author states this is false because knowledge provides a more secure repose of the soul than any belief.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the logical consequence if a belief can be doubted by its holder?
A) It becomes equal to knowledge in strength.
B) There is a difference in strength between knowledge and that belief, leading to a difference in motivation.
C) The belief cannot be compared to knowledge.
D) The belief is automatically false.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge is stronger than belief in motivation because any belief that can be doubted is inherently weaker, and even a belief that cannot be doubted lacks the security of true knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which of your core beliefs about your purpose or destiny have you ever genuinely subjected to doubt? If you haven't, does that mean they are knowledge or just comfortable beliefs?
?
You act on many beliefs daily. When you feel unmotivated to do what you claim to believe, could it be because those beliefs are not yet knowledge? What step can you take today to turn one such belief into certain knowledge?
?
The lesson says knowledge gives a more secure soul-reliance than any belief. Think of the one thing you are most certain about in your faith—is it based on compelling evidence or just habit? If it's habit, would it survive a real challenge? How would you seek the knowledge that makes it unshakable?

The Weakness of Uncertain Belief
Al-Matalib explains the weakness of non-certain belief using two arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two reasons why an uncertain (agitated) belief is weaker than knowledge in motivating action: the cause-effect principle and the possibility of being wrong.

Core Idea
Uncertain belief is weak because its cause is weak and the believer fears error.

The author now focuses on the second type of belief: the non-certain, agitated belief. He provides two clear arguments for its weakness. First, the principle that the effect follows the cause—a weak cause cannot produce a strong effect. Second, the uncertain believer must allow for the possibility of being mistaken, which naturally weakens motivation. This lesson examines those arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of belief discussed
The author describes a believer who is not certain but agitated. Read the Arabic to see the characterization.
This contrasts with the previous category.
2
Understand the cause-effect analogy
Just as a weaker cause produces a weaker effect, an uncertain belief (being a weaker cause) produces weaker motivation.
This is a rational principle.
3
Recognize the role of fear of error
The uncertain believer must allow that the truth may be contrary to his belief, which weakens his inclination to act.
This distinguishes him from the certain knower.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولمن زعم أن العلم أقوى في الدعاء إلى الفعل من الاعتقاد ، أن يقول : هذا الاعتقاد إما أن يكون بحيث لو شكك صاحبه نفسه فيه أمكنه التشكيك فيه ، أو لا يكون كذلك. فإن كان الأول فحينئذ حصل التفاوت بين العلم وبين هذا الاعتقاد في القوة ، فوجب أن يحصل التفاوت في الدعاء إلى الفعل ، لأن من المعلوم بالضرورة : أن المؤثر كلما كان أقوى ، كان التأثير أقوى ، وإن كان الثاني فحينئذ لا يبقى بين هذا الاعتقاد وبين العلم تفاوت في القوة ، وذلك معلوم البطلان بالضرورة ، لأن من المعلوم بالضرورة . أن سكون النفس إلى العلم الحق ، أيمن من سكونها إلى الاعتقاد الخالي عن الموجب وأما القسم الثاني : وهو المعتقد الذي لا يكون جازماً ، بل يكون كالمضطرب في ذلك الاعتقاد ، فنقول : إن مثل هذا الاعتقاد لا يكون مساوياً للعلم في الدعاء إلى الفعل ، بل يكون أضعف منه . وتقريره : أن الأثر فرع على المؤثر . ولما كان أحد المؤثرين أضعف من الآخر ، كان أثر الضعيف أضعف لا محالة من أثر القوي . فكان دعاء الاعتقاد الضعيف إلى الفعل أضعف من دعاء العلم القوي إليه . وأيضاً : فالعالم الجازم يكون الفعل على النفع الزائد لا يخاف فيها يعلمه أن يتغير ، وأما المعتقد المضطرب فإنه لا بد وأن يجوز أن ينكشف أن الأمر بخلاف ما اعتقده . فحيل لابد وأن يضعف ذلك الاقتضاء واعلم : أنا ربما وجدنا المبطل أو المقلد مقدم على الفعل إقداماً أقوى من إقدام العالم وذلك لا يقدح في قولنا : إن اقتضاء العلم أقوى اقتضاء من الاعتقاد ، وذلك لأن تلك القوة إنما حصلت لأمور أخرى تقارن ذلك الاعتقاد ، ولو لم يحصل إلا ذلك الاعتقاد لما حصلت تلك المزية . وتلك الأمور المقارنة بذلك الإقدام أشياء: أحدها : مدح الناس له في نصرة ذلك القول أو في الإقدام على ذلك العمل . وثانيها : أنه ربما توصل بذلك الإقدام إلى جر أنواع من المنافع العاجلة أو يتخلص بسبب ذلك الفعل من أنواع كثيرة من الذم وثالثها : أن طول الإلف والعادة قد يحمله على ذلك العمل . فهذا هو الكلام في المقايسة الحاصلة بين العلم وبين الاعتقاد
For whoever claims that knowledge is stronger in motivating action than belief, he may say: This belief is either such that if its holder doubts himself, he can cast doubt therein, or not. If the first, then there arises a difference between knowledge and this belief in strength, and necessarily a difference in motivation, because it is known by necessity that the stronger the cause, the stronger the effect. If the second, then no difference remains between this belief and knowledge in strength, and that is known to be false by necessity, because it is known by necessity that the soul's reliance on true knowledge is more secure than its reliance on belief devoid of compulsion. As for the second type: the believer who is not certain but is rather like one agitated in that belief, we say: Such belief is not equal to knowledge in motivating action; rather, it is weaker than it. Its explanation: The effect is subordinate to the cause. Since one of the causes is weaker than the other, the effect of the weak one is inevitably weaker than the effect of the strong one. So the motivation of weak belief to action is weaker than the motivation of strong knowledge to it. Also: The certain knower acts for a great benefit, not fearing that what he knows will change, whereas the agitated believer must allow that it may become clear that the matter is contrary to what he believed. Hence, inevitably, that inclination weakens. And know: We often find the misguided or the blind follower advancing towards an action with a stronger impetus than the advance of the knower, and that does not detract from our statement that the inclination of knowledge is stronger than the inclination of belief, because that strength arises from other things that accompany that belief; if nothing but that belief existed, that superiority would not occur. Those accompanying things for that advance are: First, people's praise for him in supporting that opinion or in undertaking that action. Second, that he might, through that advance, attain various kinds of immediate benefits or escape numerous types of blame. Third, that long-term familiarity and habit may drive him to that action. This is the discourse concerning the comparison between knowledge and belief.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage continues the investigation by presenting a case for those who hold that knowledge is stronger than belief in motivating action. The argument is structured around two possibilities for any belief: either it can be doubted (shakkaka nafsahu) or it cannot. If it can be doubted, then there is a real difference in strength, and thus motivation will differ proportionally. If it cannot be doubted, then no difference remains, but this is deemed false by necessity, since reliance on true knowledge is more secure than reliance on belief without compelling reason. The author then turns to the second type of belief (uncertain, agitated belief). Such belief is plainly weaker than knowledge: the effect follows the cause, so a weaker cause yields a weaker effect. Moreover, the knower acts without fear of change, whereas the agitated believer must admit the possibility of being wrong, which weakens the inclination. The author then addresses an empirical objection: we often see misguided or blindly following people acting with stronger drive than knowers. This, he explains, does not undermine the claim that knowledge's inclination is inherently stronger, because in those cases external factors amplify the motivation: praise, immediate benefits, avoidance of blame, and long-standing habit. Thus the comparison between knowledge and belief remains sound.

Key Terms
اعتقاد غير جازم (non-certain belief)
اقتضاء (inclination)
مؤثر (cause)
أثر (effect)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An uncertain belief can still motivate as strongly as knowledge if the benefits are great.
Correction: The author argues that regardless of benefits, the inherent weakness of the cause (belief) and the fear of error make motivation weaker.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the certain knower not have his motivation weakened?
A) Because his knowledge is based on evidence.
B) Because he does not fear that what he knows will change.
C) Because his belief is stronger by nature.
D) Because he receives social praise.

Key Takeaway
Uncertain belief is weaker than knowledge because its causal strength is less and because the believer's fear of error dampens motivation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, which of your beliefs about Allah are truly certain, and which are merely strong opinions? How does the certainty (or lack thereof) of each belief actually affect your daily actions? Will you commit today to identifying one area of uncertainty and seeking the evidence needed to transform it into true knowledge?
?
The passage says the uncertain believer must admit the possibility that the truth is opposite to his belief. Is there any central belief of your faith that you hold with such certainty that you never fear it could be false? If not, what does that say about the strength of your motivation to live by that belief? What specific step will you take this week to fortify that belief with decisive proof?

Explaining Apparent Counterexamples: External Factors
Al-Matalib addresses the objection from observed behavior and lists three external factors.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how external factors such as praise, immediate benefit, and habit can explain why some believers appear to act more strongly than knowers, without undermining the inherent weakness of belief.

Core Idea
Strong action from believers comes from external add-ons, not belief itself.

The author anticipates an empirical objection: we often see misguided or blindly following individuals acting with greater determination than knowledgeable people. He argues that this does not refute his claim, because such strength arises not from the belief itself but from accompanying factors. This lesson details those three factors: social praise, immediate benefits or avoidance of blame, and long-term familiarity and habit.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
The author acknowledges the empirical observation and then explains why it does not invalidate the principle.
2
Identify the three external factors
The passage lists three specific factors that can amplify motivation: people's praise, attainment of benefits or escape from blame, and prolonged familiarity/habit.
These are external to the belief itself.
3
Understand the implication
Without these factors, the inherent weakness of belief would manifest. The factors do not make belief itself stronger; they merely add extra impetus.
This preserves the original claim.

Knowledge vs. Belief: Strength and Prompting
Author argues that belief is weaker than knowledge and addresses an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why knowledge is inherently stronger than belief in prompting action and how external factors can sometimes make a false believer appear more motivated.

Core Idea
Knowledge moves the soul more than belief; strong actions from false believers come from outside.

In this passage, the author compares the motivational force of knowledge and belief. He first rejects the notion that any belief equals knowledge in strength, then examines unsettled belief and proves its weakness. Finally, he addresses an apparent counterexample: false believers acting strongly. This lesson unpacks these arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the flow: the author first dismisses equality, then explains weak belief, then answers an objection.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two arguments for weakness
Find the cause-effect argument (the effect is a branch of the cause) and the argument about the possibility of error weakening motivation. Make sure you see how each supports the claim that weak belief is weaker than knowledge.
The author uses 'Taqrīr' for the explanation of the first argument.
3
Analyze the objection and response
The author acknowledges that false believers (mubṭil) or blind followers (muqallid) sometimes act more strongly. He responds by listing three external factors. Find each factor and understand why they do not affect the inherent strength of belief.
These factors are enumerated: praise, immediate benefits, and habit.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
After considering the objection, the author reaffirms that knowledge's prompting is inherently stronger. Think about how this distinction between intrinsic and extrinsic motivation is important in Islamic epistemology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كان الثاني فحينئذ لا يبقى بين هذا الاعتقاد وبين العلم تفاوت في القوة ، وذلك معلوم البطلان بالضرورة ، لأن من المعلوم بالضرورة أن سكون النفس إلى العلم الحق أيمن من سكونها إلى الاعتقاد الخالي عن الموجب. وأما القسم الثاني : وهو المعتقد الذي لا يكون جازماً ، بل يكون كالمضطرب في ذلك الاعتقاد ، فنقول : إن مثل هذا الاعتقاد لا يكون مساوياً للعلم في الدعاء إلى الفعل ، بل يكون أضعف منه . وتقريره : أن الأثر فرع على المؤثر . ولما كان أحد المؤثرين أضعف من الآخر ، كان أثر الضعيف أضعف لا محالة من أثر القوي . فكان دعاء الاعتقاد الضعيف إلى الفعل أضعف من دعاء العلم القوي إليه . وأيضاً : فالعالم الجازم يكون الفعل على النفع الزائد لا يخاف فيه أن يتغير ، وأما المعتقد المضطرب فإنه لا بد وأن يجوز أن ينكشف أن الأمر بخلاف ما اعتقده . فحينئذ لابد وأن يضعف ذلك الاقتضاء. واعلم : أنا ربما وجدنا المبطل أو المقلد مقدم على الفعل إقداماً أقوى من إقدام العالم وذلك لا يقدح في قولنا : إن اقتضاء العلم أقوى اقتضاء من الاعتقاد ، وذلك لأن تلك القوة إنما حصلت لأمور أخرى تقارن ذلك الاعتقاد ، ولو لم يحصل إلا ذلك الاعتقاد لما حصلت تلك المزية . وتلك الأمور المقارنة بذلك الإقدام أشياء: أحدها : مدح الناس له في نصرة ذلك القول أو في الإقدام على ذلك العمل . وثانيها : أنه ربما توصل بذلك الإقدام إلى جر أنواع من المنافع العاجلة أو يتخلص بسبب ذلك الفعل من أنواع كثيرة من الذم . وثالثها : أن طول الإلف والعادة قد يحمله على ذلك العمل . فهذا هو الكلام في المقايسة الحاصلة بين العلم وبين الاعتقاد.
The second [case] would then leave no difference in strength between this belief and knowledge, and that is necessarily known to be false, because it is necessarily known that the soul's tranquility towards true knowledge is more secure than its tranquility towards belief devoid of evidence. As for the second division: the belief that is not firm but rather is like being unsettled in that belief, we say: such a belief is not equal to knowledge in prompting action, but rather is weaker. Its explanation: the effect is a branch of the cause. Since one of the two causes is weaker than the other, the effect of the weak one is necessarily weaker than the effect of the strong one. Thus the prompting of weak belief to action is weaker than the prompting of strong knowledge to it. Also: the firm knower acts for the benefit and does not fear that what he knows will change, whereas the unsettled believer must allow the possibility that the matter might be revealed to be contrary to what he believed. Therefore, that prompting necessarily weakens. And know: we sometimes find the false believer or the blind follower acting more strongly than the knower, and that does not invalidate our statement that the prompting of knowledge is stronger than the prompting of belief, because that strength is due to other things accompanying that belief; had only that belief existed, that advantage would not have been obtained. Those accompanying things prompting that action are: first, the praise of people for supporting that statement or boldness in that action; second, he might thereby obtain various types of immediate benefits or escape many kinds of blame; third, prolonged familiarity and habit might drive him to that action. This is the discussion on the comparison between knowledge and belief.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage is a comparative argument between knowledge (al-ʿilm) and belief (al-iʿtiqād) in terms of their motivational force. The author first refutes the idea that any belief could be equal to knowledge in strength, noting that true knowledge gives the soul a more secure tranquility than belief without evidence. He then examines the second type of belief—non-firm or unsettled belief—and proves it is weaker than knowledge using two arguments: (1) The cause-effect relationship: a weaker cause produces a weaker effect, so weak belief prompts action less than strong knowledge. (2) The firm knower does not fear change in the benefit, whereas the unsettled believer must allow the possibility of being wrong, which weakens motivation. The author then addresses an objection: sometimes a false believer or blind follower (al-mubṭil or al-muqallid) acts more strongly than a knower. He explains this is not a counterexample, because such strength comes from external factors (praise, immediate benefits, habit), not from the belief itself. Those factors are enumerated as three: people's praise, immediate benefits or avoidance of blame, and long familiarity or habit. Thus the conclusion stands: knowledge's prompting is inherently stronger than belief's.

مبطل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A false believer who acts more strongly than a knower proves that belief can be as strong as knowledge.
Correction: The strength comes from external incentives like praise, benefits, or habit, not from the belief itself.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why does a false believer sometimes act more strongly than a knower?
A) His belief is actually stronger than knowledge
B) External factors like praise, benefits, or habit accompany his action
C) He has no doubt in his belief
D) The action is inherently more compelling

Key Takeaway
Knowledge's intrinsic prompt is stronger than belief's; any apparent stronger action from a false believer is due to external factors like praise, benefits, or habit.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Knowledge vs. Conjecture as Motivators
Author states thesis and provides two proofs that conjecture is a weaker motivator than knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why conjecture is a weaker motivator than knowledge, based on the inherent possibility of error and observable behavioral differences.

Core Idea
Conjecture can prompt action, but knowledge prompts more strongly because it admits no possibility of error.

How do knowledge and conjecture compare when it comes to moving us to act? The author argues that although conjecture supported by evidence can motivate action like knowledge, its motivation is weaker. This lesson examines the proof for this claim, focusing on the conjecturer's awareness of possible error and the contrast in behavior between the knower and the conjecturer.

Guided Path
1
Understand the core claim
Read the statement that conjecture stands in place of knowledge as a motivator, but knowledge's prompting is stronger. This is the thesis.
2
Examine the first proof
Notice that the conjecturer allows the possibility that the matter is contrary to his belief. This prevents equality in motivational force.
Why does this matter? Because certainty (jasm) is what drives strong action.
3
Examine the second proof
Consider the behavioral example: one who knows harm avoids it consistently, while the conjecturer's avoidance varies. This illustrates the weaker motivational grip of conjecture.
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: how does this distinction affect our understanding of certainty and action in Islamic theology?
This foundation is key for later discussions on evidence and legal rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المقايسة الحاصلة بين العلم والظن . فنقول : إن الظن إذا صحب إمارته فإنه يقوم مقام العلم في كونه داعياً إلى الفعل، إلا أن دعاء العلم يكون أقوى . والدليل عليه : أن الظان يجوز أن يكون الأمر بخلاف ما ظنه ومع قيام هذا التجويز يمتنع كون الظن مساوياً للعلم في الدعاء إلى الفعل ، وأيضاً : فإنا نجد العالم باشتمال الفعل على الضرر مصراً على الاحتراز . وأما الظان فقد تختلف أحواله في كيفية ذلك الاحتراز . إذا عرفت هذا الأصل فنقول : إن مراتب الظن مختلفة في القوة والضعف ، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك وجب أن تكون مراتب تأثيراتها في الدعاء إلى الفعل مختلفة في القوة والضعف . بيان المقدمة الأولى : أن الظن الحاصل بخبر أقوام كثيرين ، أقوى من الظن الحاصل بخبر إنسان واحد . وأيضاً رؤية الأمارات أقوى في إفادة الظن، مما إذا اعتقد وجود تلك الإمارات بإخبار مخبر واحد . ومثاله : إذا شاهد ناراً بالقرب من داره، فهذا أيضاً يقتضي ظن الحرق . وإذا أخبره مخبر بوقوع نار بالقرب من داره ، فهذا أيضاً يقتضي حصول الحرق إلا أن الظن الأول أقوى ، فيثبت أن مراتب الظنون مختلفة . وإذا ثبت هذا وجب أن تكون مراتب كونها داعية إلى الفعل مختلفة ، لأنه لما كان المقتضي للدعاء إلى الفعل هو قوة الجزم، وجب أن تختلف مراتب قوة الدعاء بحسب اختلاف مراتب الظنون في القوة والضعف . وهذا هو الكلام في مقايسة العلم مع الظن .
As for the comparison between knowledge and conjecture: we say: conjecture, when accompanied by its evidence, stands in the place of knowledge as a motivator to action, except that the prompting of knowledge is stronger. The proof: the conjecturer allows the possibility that the matter is contrary to his conjecture, and with this allowance it is impossible for conjecture to be equal to knowledge in prompting action. Also: we find the one who knows that an action entails harm persistently avoids it; whereas the conjecturer's states may differ in how he avoids it. If you understand this principle, we say: the degrees of conjecture differ in strength and weakness; if it is so, then the degrees of their effects in prompting action must differ in strength and weakness. Explanation of the first premise: the conjecture derived from the report of many people is stronger than the conjecture derived from the report of a single person. Also, seeing the evidence directly is stronger in producing conjecture than believing the existence of that evidence through the report of a single informant. Example: if he sees a fire near his house, this also necessitates the conjecture of burning; if an informant tells him of a fire near his house, this also necessitates the occurrence of burning, except that the first conjecture is stronger. Thus it is established that degrees of conjectures differ. And if this is established, then the degrees of their being motivators to action must differ, because since the determinant for prompting action is the strength of certainty, the degrees of the strength of prompting must differ according to the differences in the degrees of conjectures in strength and weakness. This is the discussion on the comparison of knowledge with conjecture.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage compares knowledge (al-ʿilm) with conjecture (al-ẓann) regarding their power to prompt action. The author states that conjecture, when supported by evidence (imāra), can serve as a motivator like knowledge, but knowledge's prompting is stronger. The proof is twofold: (1) The conjecturer admits the possibility that the matter could be otherwise, which prevents conjecture from equaling knowledge in motivational force. (2) A person who knows something involves harm consistently avoids it, whereas a conjecturer's avoidance varies. Then the author introduces the principle that degrees of conjecture vary in strength, and thus their motivational effects also vary. To prove this, he gives two examples: (a) conjecture from multiple reporters is stronger than from one; (b) seeing evidence directly (like a fire) produces stronger conjecture than being told about it. Therefore, since the strength of the motivator depends on strength of certainty, the strength of prompting varies accordingly. The passage concludes that this is the full discussion on the comparison of knowledge with conjecture.

Degrees of Conjecture and Their Effects
Author explains that conjecture has degrees and their motivational effects vary accordingly.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that conjectures vary in strength, and consequently their motivational power varies proportionally.

Core Idea
Stronger conjecture produces stronger motivation to act, just as stronger light produces a brighter glow.

Not all conjectures are equal: some are stronger and some weaker. The author establishes this through clear examples, then draws the logical conclusion that stronger conjectures exert stronger influence on our actions. This lesson explores the spectrum of conjecture and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the principle
Read the author's statement: degrees of conjecture differ in strength and weakness, and thus their effects differ. This is the main thesis of this section.
2
Examine the first example
Notice the example: conjecture from many reporters is stronger than from one. This shows how evidence quantity affects strength.
3
Examine the second example
Direct observation of evidence (e.g., seeing fire) produces stronger conjecture than being told about it. This shows the impact of immediacy.
4
Connect strength to motivation
The author concludes that since strength of motivation depends on strength of certainty, differing conjecture strengths lead to differing motivational strengths.
This completes the argument started in the previous lesson.

Comparing Conjecture and Belief: Degrees of Strength
Author states conjecture may be stronger or weaker than belief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that conjecture supported by evidence can be stronger than belief, and that one must assess degrees of strength rather than assume a fixed hierarchy.

Core Idea
Conjecture with evidence can surpass belief.

In this brief but important passage, the author concludes his discussion of the three cognitive judgments—knowledge, conjecture, and belief—by comparing conjecture and belief. He makes a subtle point: conjecture is not always weaker; when supported by strong evidence, it can be stronger than belief. The key is to evaluate degrees of strength, not rely on labels.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's exact wording sink in before interpreting.
Notice the phrase 'supported by evidence' (المؤيد بالآثار)—it is key.
2
Identify the two possibilities
The author states two possibilities: conjecture may be stronger than belief, or the opposite. Find these in the text and note the conjunction 'or' (وقد يكون بالضد منه).
3
Understand the conclusion
The author concludes that one 'must consider the degrees of strength and weakness.' Ask yourself: why is this a duty? Reflect on how this applies to evaluating knowledge claims.
This connects to the learning objective about assessing degrees.

This serves as a conclusion to the series of comparisons between knowledge, conjecture, and belief.

Knowledge, Conjecture, and Belief as Motives for Action
The author compares knowledge and conjecture, then conjecture and belief as motives for action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the strength of certainty in knowledge, conjecture, and belief determines their respective motivating power for action.

Core Idea
The motivating force of a belief depends on its certainty, not its label.

This passage from al-Matalib al-‘Aliyah examines how different cognitive states—knowledge, conjecture, and belief—drive human action. The author argues that while conjecture can serve as a motive, it is weaker than knowledge because it admits the possibility of error. Moreover, conjecture itself varies in strength, and its motivational force varies accordingly. The passage also briefly compares conjecture and belief, noting that either can be stronger depending on the supporting evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the author uses a very logical, step-by-step style.
2
Identify the two comparisons
The passage has two main parts: first a comparison of knowledge and conjecture, then a comparison of conjecture and belief. Identify where the shift occurs.
The second part begins with 'أما مقايسة الظن مع الاعتقاد'.
3
Understand the proof for knowledge's superiority
The author gives two reasons: the conjecturer allows the possibility of being wrong, and we observe that a knower acts consistently while a conjecturer may vary.
This shows that knowledge produces stronger motivation because of higher certainty.
4
Grasp the principle of varying strength
The author then argues that conjecture itself has degrees, and provides examples: many witnesses vs. one, direct sight vs. report. Since motivation depends on certainty (qawwat al-jazm), the strength of motivation must vary accordingly.
This principle applies to all cognitive states: the more certain, the stronger the motive.
5
Reflect on the comparison with belief
The final part states that conjecture supported by signs may be stronger or weaker than belief, so we must evaluate each case. Consider how this connects to the overall theme.
Belief here likely means a firm conviction without necessarily being knowledge.

ظن
أما مقايسة الظن مع الاعتقاد . فاعلم أن الظن المؤيد بالآثار قد يكون أقوى من الاعتقاد، وقد يكون بالضد منه، والواجب اعتبار مراتب القوة والضعف
As for the comparison between knowledge and conjecture: we say that conjecture, when accompanied by its indication, takes the place of knowledge in being a motive for action, except that the motive of knowledge is stronger. The proof of that: the conjecturer allows that the matter may be contrary to what he conjectured, and with this allowance it is impossible for conjecture to equal knowledge in motivating action. Also, we find that the one who knows that an action involves harm is determined to avoid it, while the conjecturer may vary in how he avoids it. Once you understand this principle, we say: the levels of conjecture differ in strength and weakness, and if this is the case, then it necessarily follows that the levels of their effects in motivating action differ in strength and weakness. Explanation of the first premise: conjecture obtained from the report of many people is stronger than conjecture from the report of a single person. Also, seeing indications is stronger in producing conjecture than believing in the existence of those indications through the report of a single informant. An example: if one sees a fire near his house, this necessitates a conjecture of burning. If an informant informs him of a fire near his house, this also necessitates a conjecture of burning, except that the first conjecture is stronger. Thus it is established that the levels of conjectures differ. When this is established, it necessarily follows that their levels in being motives for action differ, because the cause of motivation is the strength of certainty, so the levels of the strength of motivation must differ according to the difference in levels of conjectures in strength and weakness. This is the discourse on the comparison of knowledge with conjecture.
Conjecture
As for the comparison of conjecture with belief: know that conjecture supported by signs may be stronger than belief, or it may be the opposite, and one must consider the levels of strength and weakness.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage is a comparative analysis of three epistemic states—knowledge (ʿilm), conjecture (ẓann), and belief (iʿtiqād)—as motives for action. It proceeds in two parts. First, the author compares knowledge and conjecture. He argues that conjecture, when supported by its proper indication, can serve as a motive for action, but it is weaker than knowledge because the conjecturer admits the possibility of error, while the knower does not. This is supported by a behavioral observation: the person who knows an action is harmful is consistently cautious, whereas a conjecturer’s caution varies. The author then establishes that conjecture itself has varying degrees of strength depending on its source—for example, testimony from many people produces stronger conjecture than testimony from one person, and direct sensory observation yields stronger conjecture than second-hand report. Since the strength of the motivation is tied to the strength of certainty (qawwat al-jazm), the varying levels of conjecture produce varying levels of motivational force. The second part briefly compares conjecture and belief, noting that conjecture supported by signs may sometimes be stronger than belief, sometimes weaker, and that one must assess each case. The passage thus teaches that motive strength is a function of the certainty of the cognitive state, not merely its type.

Key Terms
علم
ظن
اعتقاد
داعي
قوة الجزم
إمارة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that conjecture is equivalent to knowledge in motivating action because both can lead to action.
Correction: The author shows that they differ in strength due to the possibility of error in conjecture, making knowledge a stronger motive.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relationship between the strength of conjecture and its effect in motivating action?
A) Stronger conjecture always produces weaker motivation.
B) The strength of motivation is independent of the strength of conjecture.
C) The levels of motivation vary according to the levels of strength of conjecture.
D) Only knowledge, not conjecture, can motivate action.

Key Takeaway
The strength of motivation for action corresponds to the degree of certainty in the cognitive state, not merely its type.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold a conjecture about someone's intentions, do you act on it as cautiously as if you were certain? How has acting on a mere conjecture harmed a relationship? Will you now distinguish between your levels of certainty before acting?
?
The author says the knower is 'determined to avoid harm' while the conjecturer varies. Think of a sin you commit despite knowing it's harmful: is your knowledge truly certain, or have you allowed doubt to weaken your resolve? What would it take for you to become like the knower?
?
Is there an Islamic belief you hold that you act on with less conviction than a well-supported conjecture from everyday life? For example, do you take greater care to avoid a worldly loss than a spiritual one? What does that reveal about the strength of your certainty?

Only Belief, Not Objective Benefit, Motivates Action
The author refutes the view that objective benefit motivates action and proves it is belief that motivates.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that subjective belief, not objective benefit, is what truly motivates human action according to rational investigation.

Core Idea
Your beliefs about benefit, not benefit itself, drive your actions.

This chapter tackles a fundamental psychological question: what actually moves us to act? The author argues against the intuitive idea that we act because something is truly beneficial. Instead, he shows that it is our belief about benefit that does the work. This has profound implications for ethics and theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage from the source together with its English translation. Notice the flow: the author starts by stating the common view, then corrects it, then provides a proof.
Pay attention to the key terms like اعتقاد (belief) and منفعة (benefit).
2
Identify the common view the author rejects
Find where the author describes what 'people say' — that the motive for action is its being a benefit in itself. This is the view he will refute.
He calls this statement 'metaphorical' (مجازي).
3
Understand the author's correction
The author asserts that the real motive is not objective benefit but the belief that something is beneficial. He distinguishes between the metaphorical expression and its true meaning.
Contrast 'being in itself a benefit' (كونه في نفسه نفعاً) vs 'belief that it is a benefit' (اعتقاد كونه نفعاً).
4
Examine the first proof
Read the proof carefully. It sets up two scenarios: (1) belief in benefit without objective benefit leads to action; (2) objective benefit without belief does not lead to action. The conclusion is that belief is the effective factor.
The proof uses a conditional structure: if belief obtains → action; if belief absent → no action. This shows the effective factor is belief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثالث
بيان أنه لا تأثير لغير الاعتقاد والظن في الدعاء الى الفعل
اعلم أن ها هنا أشياء سوى هذه الثلاثة يجب البحث عنها . أنها هل تدعو إلى الفعل أم لا ؟
فالنوع الأول : إن الناس يقولون بحسب الظاهر : إن الداعي له إلى الفعل كونه في نفسه منفعة ومصلحة. والصارف له عن هذا الفعل كونه مضرة ومفسدة . واعلم أن هذا الكلام مجازي وتحقيقه : إن اعتقاد كونه نفعاً يدعوه إلى الفعل، واعتقاد كونه ضرراً يدعوه إلى الترك. فأما أن يقال : إن كونه في نفسه نفعاً، يدعوه إلى الفعل، وكونه من نفسه ضرراً يدعوه إلى الترك، فهذا باطل .
ويدل عليه وجوه :
الأول : إنا بينا : لو حصل اعتقاد كونه منفعة راجحة مع أنه في نفسه لا يكون كذلك في نفسه راجحاً فإنه يصير ذلك الاعتقاد داعياً إلى الفعل ولو لم يحصل هذا الاعتقاد مع أنه يحصل كونه في نفسه نفعاً راجحاً لم يصر ذلك داعياً إلى الفعل فعلمنا : أن المؤثر هو اعتقاد كونه نفعاً راجعاً . وليس المؤثر هو كونه في نفسه نفعاً راجحاً .
Chapter Three: Statement that nothing other than belief and conjecture has effect in motivating action.
Know that there are things besides these three that must be investigated: whether they motivate action or not?
The first type: people say, according to appearance, that the motive for action is that it is in itself a benefit and interest, and the deterrent from this action is that it is in itself a harm and corruption. Know that this statement is metaphorical, and its reality: the belief that it is a benefit motivates him to act, and the belief that it is a harm motivates him to refrain. As for saying that its being in itself a benefit motivates him to act, and its being in itself a harm motivates him to refrain, this is false.
Evidence for it, first: we have shown that if a belief obtains that something is a preponderant benefit even though in itself it is not preponderant, then that belief becomes a motive for action. And if this belief does not obtain, even though it is in itself a preponderant benefit, it does not become a motive for action. Thus we know that the effective factor is the belief that it is a preponderant benefit, and the effective factor is not its being in itself a preponderant benefit.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage is a chapter heading and the beginning of a refutation. The author aims to prove that only subjective states—belief (iʿtiqād) and conjecture (ẓann)—have any influence in motivating or deterring action, not objective properties of actions themselves. He first identifies a common misconception: people often say that an action’s own benefit (manfaʿa) or harm (maḍarra) is what motivates or deters. The author declares this statement to be metaphorical, and he provides its true interpretation: it is actually the belief that something is beneficial or harmful that motivates or deters. The statement that the action’s own nature directly causes motivation is false. He then offers the first proof: if a person believes an action to be a preponderant benefit, even if it is not objectively so, that belief alone suffices to motivate action. Conversely, if the same action is objectively a preponderant benefit but the person does not believe it to be so, it will not motivate. Therefore, the effective factor (al-muʾaththir) is the belief, not the objective reality. This is a foundational principle in the psychology of action from a kalam perspective.

ظن
منفعة
مضرة
داعي
مؤثر
مجازي

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many people think that the objective benefit or harm of an action itself is what directly motivates or deters them.
Correction: The author shows that it is actually one's belief about the benefit or harm that is the effective motive; the objective property has no direct motivational influence.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof in this chapter, what is the true effective factor (al-muʾaththir) in motivating action?
A) The action being objectively beneficial or harmful in itself.
B) The belief that the action is beneficial or harmful.
C) The strength of the indication (amāra) supporting the belief.
D) The number of informants reporting the benefit.

Key Takeaway
Only belief or conjecture about benefit or harm has actual motivational force, not the objective reality of benefit or harm.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a decision you made recently that required sacrifice—like waking for Fajr, giving charity, or avoiding a sin. What belief about benefit or harm actually drove that action? Was it belief in an unseen reward or fear of punishment? Now, reflect on a decision you regret: what false belief about benefit led you to it? How can you strengthen true beliefs to align your actions with what is truly beneficial for your akhirah?
?
This lesson states that even if something is objectively beneficial, without belief it cannot motivate you. What is one Islamic practice you know is beneficial (e.g., night prayer, reading Qur'an, lowering the gaze) but you struggle to perform? What belief about its benefit do you lack? Write down one concrete step you will take this week to turn your intellectual knowledge into genuine belief so that it moves you to action.
?
Consider a habitual sin you commit despite knowing its harm. According to this passage, the real cause is your belief (or conjecture) that it brings some benefit—perhaps pleasure, relief, or social acceptance. What specific benefit do you believe that sin gives you? Examine whether that benefit is real or illusory. What true benefit of avoiding that sin (e.g., Allah's pleasure, self-respect, lasting peace) can you focus on to overpower that false belief? Commit to a du'ā' asking Allah to replace the false belief with true conviction.

Belief as the True Motivator: First Proof
Al-Razi presents the first proof that belief, not intrinsic benefit, is the effective cause of action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof: belief in benefit motivates action even when the action is not actually beneficial, and lack of belief prevents motivation even when the action is beneficial.

Core Idea
Belief, not objective benefit, is what moves us to act.

This lesson begins al-Razi's refutation of the common view that intrinsic benefit directly motivates action. He clarifies that ordinary speech about benefit is metaphorical. The first proof demonstrates that belief in benefit can motivate independently of actual benefit, and vice versa.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the literal translation. Note the author's use of 'metaphorical speech'.
2
Understand the correction
Al-Razi corrects the common view: the real motivator is belief, not objective benefit. Identify the sentence where he states this.
Look for 'تحقيقه : إن اعتقاد كونه نفعاً يدعوه إلى الفعل'.
3
Analyze the first proof
The first proof is a conditional: if belief in benefit occurs without real benefit, action occurs; if real benefit occurs without belief, no action. This shows that belief is the effective factor.
The key conclusion: 'فعلمنا : أن المؤثر هو اعتقاد كونه نفعاً راجحاً'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثالث بيان أنه لا تأثير لغير الاعتقاد والظن في الدعاء الى الفعل اعلم أن ها هنا أشياء سوى هذه الثلاثة يجب البحث عنها . أنها هل تدعو إلى الفعل أم لا ؟ فالنوع الأول : إن الناس يقولون بحسب الظاهر : إن الداعي له إلى الفعل كونه في نفسه منفعة ومصلحة. والصارف له عن هذا الفعل كونه مضرة ومفسدة . واعلم أن هذا الكلام مجازي وتحقيقه : إن اعتقاد كونه نفعاً يدعوه إلى الفعل ، واعتقاد كونه ضرراً يدعوه إلى الترك. فأما أن يقال : إن كونه في نفسه نفعاً ، يدعوه إلى الفعل ، وكونه من نفسه ضرراً يدعوه إلى الترك ، فهذا باطل . ويدل عليه وجوه : الأول : إنا بينا : لو حصل اعتقاد كونه منفعة راجحة مع أنه في نفسه لا يكون كذلك في نفسه راجحاً ( فإنه يصير ذلك الاعتقاد داعياً إلى الفعل ولو لم يحصل هذا الاعتقاد مع أنه يحصل كونه في نفسه نفعاً راجحاً ) (١) لم يصر ذلك داعياً إلى الفعل فعلمنا : أن المؤثر هو اعتقاد كونه نفعاً راجعاً . وليس المؤثر هو كونه في نفسه نفعاً راجحاً . والثاني : إن الفاعل إذا فعل لأجل غرض . فذلك الغرض إنما يحصل في آخر ذلك الفعل . مثاله : أن من يبني بيتاً لدفع الحر والبرد ، فهذا المقصود إنما يحصل بعد دخول البيت ، بكماله في الوجود . ولهذا قيل : إن أول الفكر آخر العمل . إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : كون الفاعل فاعلاً لذلك الفعل متقدم على دخول ذلك الغرض في الوجود، والمتقدم على الشيء يمتنع كونه معللاً بالمتأخر الثالث : إن حصول ذلك الغرض معلل يكنون الفاعل فاعلاً لذلك الشيء الذي هو السبب الحصول ذلك النفع ، فلو كان ذلك الغرض علة لتلك الفاعلية لزم الدور ، وهو محال فيثبت بهذه الوجوه : أن كون الفعل منفعة وحسناً ، ليس علة لكون فاعلاً له البتة . وإنما المؤثر في الفاعلية هو علم الفاعل باشتمال ذلك الفعل على ذلك الوجه من الحسن والمنفعة .
Chapter Three: Explanation that nothing other than belief and opinion influences the calling to action. Know that there are things besides these three which must be investigated: whether they call to action or not. The first type: People say apparently that what calls him to action is its being in itself a benefit and advantage. And what turns him away from this action is its being a harm and corruption. Know that this speech is metaphorical, and its reality is: his belief that it is a benefit calls him to action, and his belief that it is a harm calls him to abandonment. As for saying that its being in itself a benefit calls him to action, and its being in itself a harm calls him to abandonment, this is false. Evidence for it is by proofs: First: We have shown: if belief that it is a preponderant benefit occurs, while in itself it is not preponderant, then that belief becomes a caller to action; and if this belief does not occur, though its being in itself a preponderant benefit occurs, it does not become a caller to action. So we know that the effective thing is belief that it is a preponderant benefit, not its being in itself a preponderant benefit. Second: The agent, when he acts for an end, that end only occurs at the end of that action. Example: whoever builds a house to repel heat and cold, this purpose only occurs after the house enters fully into existence. Hence it is said: the beginning of thought is the end of action. If this is established, we say: The agent's being an agent of that action precedes the entrance of that end into existence, and what precedes a thing cannot be caused by what is later. Third: The occurrence of that end is caused by the agent's being an agent of that thing which is the cause of obtaining that benefit. If that end were the cause of that agency, circularity would follow, which is impossible. So by these proofs it is established that the action's being a benefit and good is not at all a cause for his being an agent of it. Rather, the effective thing in agency is the agent's knowledge that the action includes that aspect of goodness and benefit.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that the intrinsic benefit or harm of an action itself directly motivates or deters the agent. The author (likely Fakhr al-Din al-Razi in his Al-Matalib al-Aliyah) begins by stating the chapter's theme: only belief and opinion influence calling to action, not other things. He then identifies three types of things that are commonly thought to influence action; the first type is the intrinsic benefit or harm. He explains that common speech is metaphorical; the real cause is the agent's belief in benefit or harm. To prove this, he gives three arguments. The first argument is that belief in benefit can motivate even when the action is not actually beneficial, and absence of belief can prevent motivation even when the action is intrinsically beneficial. The second argument uses the temporal priority of the agent over the end: the end occurs after the action, so it cannot be the cause of the action. The third argument points out the circularity if the end caused the agency, since agency causes the end. The conclusion is that the action's being good or beneficial is not the cause; rather, the agent's knowledge of that goodness is the effective factor.

Key Terms
اعتقاد
منفعة
داعٍ
صارف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Intrinsic benefit or harm of an action directly compels the will.
Correction: The first proof shows that belief can motivate without actual benefit, so the real cause is belief.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, what happens if someone believes an action is beneficial even though it is not actually beneficial?
A) They will not act because it is not actually beneficial.
B) They will act because of their belief.
C) They will act only if the benefit is real.
D) The belief has no effect.

Key Takeaway
The first proof confirms that belief in benefit, not the objective benefit, is what actually moves an agent to act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you last did something you knew was wrong, was it because you believed at that moment it would bring you some benefit? How does knowing that belief is the real motivator force you to examine the beliefs behind your sins?
?
What belief about yourself – perhaps that you cannot change, or that you are not responsible – is actually preventing you from acting on what you know is truly beneficial? Will you let that belief remain, or will you actively replace it with the truth?
?
If your actions are driven by your beliefs, then every wrong action you commit is evidence of a wrong belief you hold. Which belief in your heart is the most dangerous one right now, and what specific step will you take today to uproot it?

Second Proof: The End Cannot Precede the Action
Al-Razi presents the second proof based on temporal priority: the end cannot cause the action because the action precedes the end.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof: the agent's action must precede the end, so the end cannot be the cause of the action.

Core Idea
The end occurs after the action, so it cannot cause the action.

The second proof uses the temporal relationship between the agent and the end. When someone acts for a purpose, that purpose is achieved only after the action is complete. Therefore, the purpose cannot be the cause of the action, as it comes later.

Guided Path
1
Read the second proof
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to the example of building a house to repel heat and cold.
2
Understand the temporal argument
The proof states that the agent's being an agent of the action is prior to the occurrence of the end. Since a cause must precede its effect, the end cannot be the cause of the action.
Key phrase: 'كون الفاعل فاعلاً لذلك الفعل متقدم على دخول ذلك الغرض في الوجود'.
3
Grasp the implication
This shows that the end (the benefit) is not the cause of the action; rather, something else (the agent's knowledge) must be the cause.
This proof refutes the idea that intrinsic benefit motivates.

Source Text

Chapter Three: Explanation that nothing other than belief and opinion influences the calling to action. Know that there are things besides these three which must be investigated: whether they call to action or not. The first type: People say apparently that what calls him to action is its being in itself a benefit and advantage. And what turns him away from this action is its being a harm and corruption. Know that this speech is metaphorical, and its reality is: his belief that it is a benefit calls him to action, and his belief that it is a harm calls him to abandonment. As for saying that its being in itself a benefit calls him to action, and its being in itself a harm calls him to abandonment, this is false. So we know that the effective thing is belief that it is a preponderant benefit, not its being in itself a preponderant benefit. If that end were the cause of that agency, circularity would follow, which is impossible. How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The lesson teaches that the agent's being an agent precedes the end. In your daily choices, do you often focus more on the anticipated result than on the act itself? How can you align your intention to be present in the act rather than fixated on a future outcome?
